Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ dies sanguinis ♥ In 204 BCE, the Anatolian goddess Cybele was formally added to the Roman pantheon. Worship of the deity included ...a day known as dies sanguinis, or Day of Blood, in which priests (and perhaps lay-worshippers, it is not very clear) whipped themselves to the point of bleeding. Some celebrants event castrated themselves, replicating the events of their cult's founding myth. This day fell on the 24th of March every year, and was part of a wider festival celebrating the goddess [364].

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Jill Levine; Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Editor ♣ ♥


Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ [430] Military power was typically held by two commanders called consuls who were elected by a military assembly called the comitia centuriata, except for periods when the consuls themselves appointed a dictator. Consuls were always drawn from a select group of aristocrats (patricians) until 367 BCE when plebeians could stand for office.[431] Consuls presided over and acted in collaboration with a powerful council of nobles called the Senate[432] - such as on decisions to go to war - and by convention usually implemented their decrees.[433] Senators were elected by the comitia centuriata and consisted of the same select patrician class until it too included plebeians in the later period.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ [434] New laws were initiated by a class of lawmakers called magistrates (who could be consuls, praetors or tribunes) but were prepared and advised by the Senate which in effect controlled Roman legislation. Magistrates were themselves Senators; elected to their position for one year, they could hold a number of magistracies during their lifetime, and simultaneously other offices, such as priest.[435] Roman legislation became law on the agreement of the relevant assembly under its jurisdiction (comitia centuriata, comitia tributa, concilium plebis) that was asked to vote on it by the magistrate. Laws passed by a comitia 'were known as leges (sing. lex), and those by the concilium plebis were called plebiscita (sing. plebiscitum) (decrees of the plebeians).'[436] Male citizens voted on legislation and elected government officials in popular assemblies in an oral manner until 139 BCE when secret ballots were introduced.[437] Assemblies could not formally discuss legislation but informal public discussions were held before votes, which women, slaves and foreigners could attend.[438] For a long time the comitia centuriata was the principal legislative body but it rarely legislated after 218 BC.[439] The Senate, always influential in decisions of war, diplomacy and religious activities, grew especially in power after the Second Punic War.[440] By the Late Roman Republic - the 2nd and 1st centuries BCE - 'the Senate was the virtual government of Rome'. Whilst it did not make laws the Senatorial decree 'senatus consultum ultimum' was used frequently as an 'enabling act' to allow magistrates 'to employ every means possible to restore order.' The last such order was employed in 40 BCE.[441] A fourth assembly known as the comitia curiata represented the three aristocratic tribes and "conferred imperium on consuls and praetors" but had no legislative powers.[442]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ "ius honorarium, the law contained in the Edict of the praetor, who, under the Republic and Early Empire administered law in Rome; this form of law derived its name from the praetor's magistracy (honos) and was held to 'assist, supplement or amend' the ius civile.".[443] The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[444] However, the priesthood's role in the affairs of Roman society gradually declined through the 509-264 BCE period: the priests "originally interpreted civil law, ... this role was taken over by jurists (mainly of senatorial rank), and praetors [who] administered the law and issued edicts."[445] Professional judges did not exist until the Roman Dominate although at that time their precise role vis-a-vis that of Imperial officials is a matter of debate. [446] Before this time there were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.


Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred present ♥ inferred from continuity with earlier periods and in later; no clear evidence of change.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ Consuls could critique each others' decisions and "in case of conflict, the negative voice prevailed".[447] Consuls always acted in collaboration with the senate.[448] The Senate "prepared legislation to be put before the assemblies, administered finances, dealt with foreign relations and supervised state religion. In the 2nd and 1st centuries BC the Senate was the virtual government of Rome ... It could not make laws but issued decrees ... The senatus consultum ultimum ... last employed in 40 BC ... authorized magistrates to employ every means possible to restore order."[449] Probably "from a very early stage, some sort of right of appeal of the people against the possible arbitrariness of superior magistrates ... The intercessio from a colleague or a tribune of the plebs was another mechanism to assure some balance of power, aimed at preventing abuses and favoring reaching consensus."[450]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ present ♥ In 300 BCE "the right to appeal to the whole people (provocatio ad populum) against decisions of consuls and other magistrates was guaranteed; this law was said to have reinforced earlier laws of 509 and 449."[451] According to tradition, from the beginning: "A Roman citizen now generally had the possibility of appeal (provocatio) to the people against a consul who exercised his power in the area enclosed by the pomerium plus one mile beyond."[452] "Commanders in the field did not have their imperium thus restricted until the 'Porcian Laws' sometime in the second century B.C."[453]

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ present ♥"from a very early stage, some sort of right of appeal of the people against the possible arbitrariness of superior magistrates ... The intercessio from a colleague or a tribune of the plebs was another mechanism to assure some balance of power, aimed at preventing abuses and favoring reaching consensus."[454]
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ Consuls always acted in collaboration with the senate.[455] Probably "from a very early stage, some sort of right of appeal of the people against the possible arbitrariness of superior magistrates ... The intercessio from a colleague or a tribune of the plebs was another mechanism to assure some balance of power, aimed at preventing abuses and favoring reaching consensus."[456] Annual succession (term period of the magistrates) might be considered a constraint on the executive.[457] Collegiate nature of rule might be considered a constraint: "it is difficult to find any early Roman magistracy which is not collegiate, in the simple sense of being comprised of more than one individual. Even the dictatorship, the least collegiate of all Roman offices, is both inextricable from the office of the magister equitum, and circumscribed by various restrictions. The praetorship after 367 would stand out here, although not if regarded as inextricably linked with the consulship."[458] After 494 BCE "the powers of the plebeian tribunes would encroach further on the consuls' exercise of imperium. Indeed the tribunes had the power of veto against all regular magistrates, but only in Rome itself."[459] Mid 5th century BCE consular tribunate (abolished 367 BCE): "each of a year's consular tribunes had veto power over other individual members of his college."[460] 287 BCE a "law was passed reaffirming that all citizens were to be subject to plebiscites."[461] In 339 BCE a plebeian dictator "passed serveral progressive measures, one of which ... made plebiscita ... binding on the whole people" including the powerful families, a law which had previously (apparently unsuccessfully?) been ruled in 449 BCE.[462]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ present ♥ In 300 BCE "the right to appeal to the whole people (provocatio ad populum) against decisions of consuls and other magistrates was guaranteed; this law was said to have reinforced earlier laws of 509 and 449."[463] According to tradition, from the beginning: "A Roman citizen now generally had the possibility of appeal (provocatio) to the people against a consul who exercised his power in the area enclosed by the pomerium plus one mile beyond."[464] "Commanders in the field did not have their imperium thus restricted until the 'Porcian Laws' sometime in the second century B.C."[465]
♠ Impeachment ♣ present ♥ consules could be removed from power for negligence, by censors after 3rd c BCE [466]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ present ♥ The patricians "were supported by their clients and retainers seems clear; otherwise their numerical inferiority to the combined forces of the other citizens of Rome would have made it impossible for them to withstand political opposition."[467] Patricians likely became "less successful in forcing their will on their retainers."[468]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ "there was a rough system of informal and formal checks and balances in place that worked well enough over a period of some centuries to make figures such as Sulla and Caesar outsized exceptions. The simple principle that the empowered should operate a measure of self-restraint in the interest of political harmony (concordia) operated as a suprisingly effacious force down to the end of the Republic."[469] "In the later Republic, the knights (or equites) - the wealthy non-office-holding arm of the Roman ruling class" had influence in Rome.[470] "The power of the patricians was steadily eroded so that by c. 300 B.C. the political advantages of being a patrician were slight."[471]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ unknown ♥

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ An array of religious institutions and individuals determined what the Roman gods wanted (e.g. college of augurs) and religious offices (duum viri) also were dedicated to interpreting sacred texts known as the Libri Sibyllini that the Romans considered prophetic (retained from the regal period) so which was effectively an ever-present critique. Romans considered infrastructure dedicated to reading omens and interpreting divine will necessary because every major action of the Roman state had to be legitimated by a reading of the public auspices (the signs of Jupiter).[472]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ augures and preists who interpreted divine will made recomendations to Senate, seems to have included advising against certain actions
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ "various boards of priests" had some influence in Rome.[473] The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[474] The vestal virgins had many privileges: "Wills and treaties were in their keeping, and they themselves could make a will. They could conduct business in their own name. They could give evidence in court without taking an oath. ... If they accidentally met a criminal on his way to execution, he was spared."[475]; "any injury to them was punishable by death; they could own and administer their own property ...; when they went out they were preceded by a lictor and had complete right of way on the streets; they could even drive in carriages within the city limits (otherwise only permitted to empresses)."[476]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ An array of religious institutions and individuals determined what the Roman gods wanted (e.g. college of augurs) and religious offices (duum viri) also were dedicated to interpreting sacred texts known as the Libri Sibyllini that the Romans considered prophetic (retained from the regal period) so which was effectively an ever-present critique. Romans considered infrastructure dedicated to reading omens and interpreting divine will necessary because every major action of the Roman state had to be legitimated by a reading of the public auspices (the signs of Jupiter).[477] From c300 BCE "Although the pontiffs continued to be extremely important as expert interpreters of the law, both before and after the passage of the Ogulnian Law, Cn. Flavius's actions [publication of the legis actiones and the legal calendar] simply removed any lingering pretense that the patrician college of pontiffs exercised a monopolistic control of the civil law. Like the increase in number of priests responsible for consulting the Sibylline Books in 368 B.C., this augmentation resulted from the fact that in Roman aristocratic culture these prominent priesthoods were viewed as being akin to public magistracies and as bestowing similar status and prestige. Roman society was becoming increasingly secularized, Roman religion was coming more and more under the influence of Greek culture, and these two trends were seriously eroding the traditional patrician claim to special expert religious knowledge. High-ranking plebeians, who were peers of the most prominent patricians in every other way, were eager to become members of these exclusive religious clubs and thereby to top off their public careers with the added distinction of a priestly office."[478] Public auspices ritual "entailed the competence to request, observe, and announce Jupiter's signs regarding an important act and then to complete what was intended. Since auspication preceded every major action taken on the state's behalf, it formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power."[479]
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ seems to have been restricted to political officials (censores), though was no clera distinction between secular/sacred authority among Roman officials
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ auguries attest to importance of acting in accordance with will of gods

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ absent ♥ Consusls elected by the comitia centuriata: "commanders of military forces ... presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office." Their minimum age "increased to 42 in the 1st century BC)."[480] In the Roman electoral system voting was by group. Each group reached its own decision, then "the decision of the assembly was the decision of the majority of the groups." Thus richer groups, less numerous, had disproportionate say[481]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ present ♥ Consusls elected by the comitia centuriata: "commanders of military forces ... presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office." Their minimum age "increased to 42 in the 1st century BC)."[482] In the Roman electoral system voting was by group. Each group reached its own decision, then "the decision of the assembly was the decision of the majority of the groups." Thus richer groups, less numerous, had disproportionate say[483]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 102 ♥
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 11 ♥ Tribune Tiberius Gracchus who initiated land reforms was assassinated some time after 133 BCE.[484] Gaius Gracchus another tribune who attempted to pass agricultural reforms was "declared a public enemy and was put to death along with 3,000 supporters" 121 BCE.[485] Period known as Sulla's civil wars had five. 1. Sulla's election was contested by Marius and a mob of people. Sulla fled Rome and Marius took power. 2. Sulla returned with an army to contest this and defeated Marius and reestablished consulship. 3. Sulla removed from office while he was out on campaign by Senate who inited Marius back 4. Marius died, passed consulship on to Cinna who was murdered in a munity of troops. 5. Sulla returned with army and defeated Marius's son. 6. Conspiracy of Catiline.[486] 7. Caesar crosses Rubicon 49 BCE, becomes dictator after civil war.[487] 8. Caesar assassinated 44 BCE.[488] 9. Senate declares Mark Anthony - official head of state - enemy 43 BCE.[489] 10. Octavian takes consulship by force 43 BCE.[490] 11. Octavian and Mark Anthony defeat republicans Brutus and Cassius.[491]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 9 ♥ There were a number of these in this period. E.g. period known as Sulla's civil wars alone had five. 1. Sulla's election was contested by Marius and a mob of people. Sulla fled Rome and Marius took power. 2. Sulla returned with an army and defeated Marius and reestablished consulship. 3. Sulla removed from office while he was out on campaign by Senate who inited Marius back 4. Marius died, passed consulship on to Cinna who was murdered in a munity of troops. 5. Sulla returned with army and defeated Marius's son. 6. Caesar crosses Rubicon 49 BCE, becomes dictator after civil war.[492] 7. 44 BCE Caeser assassinated.[493] 8. Senate declares Mark Anthony - official head of state - enemy and defeated him 43 BCE.[494] 9. Octavian takes consulship by force 43 BCE.[495]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ [496]


Need to edit this The Roman Republic was ruled by a military and religious aristocracy which maintained an extremely complex set of religious and political institutions that with no written constitution[497] significantly evolved over the 509-264 BCE period in the aftermath of the monarchy (509-367 BCE) and then as the city-state of Rome conquered and began to rule foreign peoples and territories. The aristocracy used voting assemblies to elect two ruling officials called magesteries who appointed an aristocratic assembly known as the Senate which instructed or advised (in the form of decrees) the executive. Magistrates had "executive, judicial, legislative, diplomatic, military and even religious functions."[498] Consuls, elected by the comitia centuriata (assembly of military), were "commanders of military forces ... [who] presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office"[499] and could appoint a single dictator for a six-month period for times of crisis.[500] Consuls always acted in collaboration with the senate.[501] For Roman law-making the voting assembly comitia centuriata was the "main legislative body [which] passed laws (leges) named after the magistrates who proposed them. After 287 BC the people controlled legislation with their resolutions (plebiscita). The comitia centuriata rarely legislated after 218 BC."[502]; additionally, "edicts of magistrates with imperium and interpretations of jurists ... had the force of law", whilst "Senatorial decrees (senatus consulta ...)... were strictly only advice to magistrates but were generally binding"[503]. "As Rome expanded, more magistrates were needed, and the consuls lost some of their original functions to other magistrates."[504] From the 318 BCE plebiscitum Ovinium, censors, two magesteries elected by the comitia centuriata who had "especially important sacral and civic duties",[505] replaced the two consuls as the individuals with the authority to appoint and remove senators, and they also appointed new consuls as senators. The Roman magesteries reflect both the aristocratic nature of Roman political organization which could permit powerful individuals but did not enable a single individual to dominate Roman society: "The term of office for magistrates was one year, and a senator would expect to be elected to at least two or three magistracies during his lifetime. A magistrate could hold other offices simultaneously, such as a priesthood."[506] It is speculated that the three ruling tribes or families (patricians) that ruled the Roman commoners (plebeians) during the regal period, before the tyranny, may have again had political significance during the historically murkier 509-367 BCE period; in that the aristocratic rulers may have wished to create a political system whereby one family could never dominate.[507] In the consulship "no two individuals of the same gens ever held office contemporaneously" but in the brief consular tribunate "there [were] instances of the same gens holding more than one office."[508] "There is, in other words, no instance where one gens was able to dominate in any year, and we know that laws were passed later in the republic to insist on distribution of office in the priesthood on exactly these grounds."[509] In this respect, perhaps, it may be interesting that the Roman Republic had no independent central bureaucracy; it was not until the empire period that revenues were diverted into an imperial treasury[510]. The state treasury - cash and Senate archives - was in fact under the custody of a powerful priesthood inside the temple of Saturn controlled by elected officials called quaestors[511]. The Pontifex Maximus, chief priest of the college of pontifices, was also an elected position - elected by the comitia tributa (assembly of all citizens). The pontifice lead the state cult and had to "maintain the pax deorum, the 'peace with the gods'"[512]. The location of the Roman treasury was thus a symbolic keystone for the Roman Republic because the all-important public auspices rituals "formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power"[513]; in theory held by the executive and first consuls from 509 CE,[514] in practice managed by specialist elected priests and full-time priestesses (Vestals, supported by the state and "full-time professional clergy, along with the priestess of Ceres and Proserpina"[515]) and an array of religious institutions that determined what the Roman gods wanted (e.g. college of augurs) and attempted to pleased them through ritual sacrifices and maintainence of their temples (e.g. vestals and flamines). Religious offices (duum viri) also were dedicated to interpreting sacred texts known as the Libri Sibyllini that the Romans considered prophetic (retained from the regal period). Romans considered infrastructure dedicated to reading omens and interpreting divine will necessary because every major action of the Roman state had to be legitimated by a reading of the public auspices (the signs of Jupiter).[516]. This gave the Roman priesthood an important influence over Roman society. Until Lex Ogulnia (300 BCE) all pontifices were patricians.[517] However, the direct role of the priesthood in the affairs of Roman society gradually declined through the 509-264 BCE period: the priests "originally interpreted civil law, ... this role was taken over by jurists (mainly of senatorial rank), and praetors [who] administered the law and issued edicts."[518] The famous Twelve Tables of 451-450 BCE was "drawn up by a board of ten men (decemviri legibusscribundis ...)".[519] An important trend in the nature of Roman government in the Early Republic was the rise of the plebeian class to positions of power and status. This particularly accellerated from about 444 BCE when began a period historians have called the "Conflict of the Orders". The tribuni militum consulari potestate (consular tribunate) emerged briefly during this time of disruption (to be abolished 367 BCE). "Between 444 and 392, consuls were often replaced by three, four, or six military tribunes with consular power (consular tribunes) and in all years between 391 and 367 by six consular tribunes."[520] This was an elected office that could be held both by members of the powerful families and the Roman citizens.[521] "Here the Romans devised a college of up to six magistrates who had the consular 'power' to lead an army yet who did not have imperium and whose auspices were deficient in some way. The idea perhaps was to keep members of the plebeian class - who were eligible for the office - away from the highest public auspices."[522] Nevertheless, after the consular tribunate was abolished by the lex Licinia Sextia "and the consular constitution was reestablished for 366; henceforth plebeians were eligible for election to one of the consulships, and in more than half the years between 366 and 342 a plebeian was elected."[523] Due to the murky nature of the sources, however, we can only be certain of much of what we know about the structure of Roman rule such as "a double magistry and a conflict between ruling classes (patrcians) and Roman citizens (plebeians)" from about 367 BCE.[524]


Institutions of the Roman Republic

c270 BCE "most of the classic institutions of the middle Republic, as described by Polybius in the second century, were in place."[525]
the most recent best sources of information about them: Consulate, Senate (Bonnefond-Coudry 1989, Ryan 1998), popular assemblies (Farrell 1986, Yakobson 1999, Pina Polo 1989, 1996, Laser 1997, Millar 1998, Sandberg 2001, Morstein-Marx 2004), aedileship (Sabbatucci 1954), tribunate of the plebs (Lobrano 1982, Sancho 1984, Thommen 1989), censorship (Astin 1982, 1985), dictatorship (Hartfield 1982, Hurlet 1993), praetorship (Brennan 2000).[526]

comitia centuriata (Centuriate Assembly)

Originally the army. "Under a consul (or, theoretically, a praetor), the assembly passed important legislative bills (rogationes) into law (lex, plural leges) and voted on war and peace). Under a consul (or dictator, interrex, or consular tribune), it elected consuls, praetors, and censors. Under a consul or praetor (after the assembly was convened, 'lending' auspices to the tribune of the plebs), it conducted popular trials if the penalty was death."[527]

Comitia curiata (Curiate Assembly).

"gave 'military auspices' to consuls and to praetors once they were elected by the centuriate assembly, and also to dictators and nonmagistrates cum imperio."[528]

comitia tributa - Tribunes and concilium plebis

- assembly of all citizens. divided into voting districts. In the Regal period the king's power was his 'imperium' and in theory this enabled him to do whatever he wished. In practice, he consulted with a consilium (advisory body of aristocrats) which later became the 'senate'. According to tradition the king "also sought the (well-organised) approval of the people in assembly (populus)"[529]
comitia tributa. Tribal Assembly. "After 241 B.C., there were four tribes in Rome itself and thirty-one around the city. New territory, as added, was incorporated administratively into existing tribal units; freedmen, however, were restricted to the four urban tribes. Consuls and praetors presided in this assembly, where individuals had equal votes within their tribes and a majority of tribes determined decisions. The assembly voted on legislation, elected minor magistrates, and conducted some popular trials if the penalty was a fine. Under the lex Domitia of 104 B.C. (suspended 81-63 B.C.), seventeen tribes chosen by lot elected members of the principal colleges of priests."[530]
From 494 BCE, after a protest/strike against debt-bondage (nexum),[531] Roman citizens were permitted to elect two leaders called tribunes (their number later increased to 10).[532] and have an assembly known as the concilium plebis.[533] This citizen assembly was able to try the ruling families for crimes against ordinary Romans.[534]
Concilium Plebis or Comitia Tributa - "the people organized by tribus, areas of domicile." tribunes of the plebs - top plebeian official. "The tribunes became for all practical purposes officials of the Roman state."[535]

Senate

members appointed by consuls (by censors from the 318 BCE plebiscitum Ovinium, who also appointed new consuls to senate) and could be removed/impeached by the same official. The senate was an institution which advised the magistrates.[536] 300 members before 81 BCE, 600 until 45 BCE, then 900 until

Augustus reduced it to 600.[537] What the senate decided (senatus consultum) in theory only guidance (could be vetoed by a consul or tribune whence it became a patrum auctoritas) but in practice "long guided state administration and policy in almost all matters, including wars, allocation of provinces, (eventually) all extensions of imperium, triumphs, the state religion, and finance; it also engaged in preliminary discussion of legislative bills."[538]

Quaestors

elected position. "financial and administrative officials who maintained public records, administered the treasury (aerarium), acted as paymasters accompanying generals on campaigns and were financial secretaries to governors."[539] In 2nd century BCE the quaestorship (a provincial appointment?) "was an entry-level office; it had limited powers, and in this period was usually held around age 30."[540]

Censors

"Before 387 B.C., when the territory of recently conquered Veii was annexed and formed into four new tribes (Livy 6.5.8), Roman territory had been divided into twenty-one tribes. The city itself formed four urban tribes, and the surrounding countryside was organized into seventeen rustic tribes. Membership of these geographical tribes was assigned by censors. Although the office of censor was created in 443 B.C., it was not until the late fourth century B.C. that the Romans began to elect censors regularly about every five years. During the early history of the office (443-38 B.C.), censors were elected on average about every nine years (Austin 1982). The censors were always two in number and held office for eighteen months. Their primary duty was to conduct a census of the Roman people, which involved taking statements under oath from adult male heads of households as to the members of their family and their property holdings; on the basis of these formal declarations, the censors assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property. This entire nexus of the comitia centuriata, census, tribal organization, and state taxation was attributed to King Servius Tullius by the ancient tradition."[541]
elected position. Two magistrates "that involved some especially important sacral and civic duties".[542] The office of the censor (censorship) from 443 BCE but not always present were two officials who enrolled citizens into military service.[543] In 351 BCE the first censors was elected from outside the ruling families.[544] c339 BCE lex Publilia ruled that one censor should be a plebeian.[545]

Aediles

elected by comitia tributa. "Two plebeian magistrates administered temple of Ceres, function later "extended to public buildings and archives (of the plebiscita and senatus consulta). From 367 BC two curule aediles were elected from the patricians. The plebeian and curule aediles had similar functions at Rome; they were in charge of the maintenance and repair of public buildings (such as temples, roads and aqueducts), of markets (especially weights and measures), of the annona (to the time of Julius Caesar), and of public games and festivals (to the time of Augustus, when games were transferred to praetors)."[546]

Praetor

elected by comitia centuriata. "In 366 BC the praetor urbanus (city praetor) was introduced, who was almost exclusively concerned with the administration of law at Rome. The praetor ... was the supreme civil judge. By the middle republic the praetors' powers were restricted to law and justice... By 241 BC a second praetor ... was established to deal with legal cases in which one or both parties were foreigners. ... there were eight by 80 BC. Praetors issued annual edicts that were an important source of Roman law."[547] "A third magistracy, the praetorship, was also established in 367."[548] The first plebeian praetor was in 336 BCE.[549]

Consuls

elected by the comitia centuriata. "commanders of military forces ... presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office." Their minimum age "increased to 42 in the 1st century BC)."[550] A praetor maximus might have been a principal magistrate "at the head of the newly born republic."[551] According to Alexander Bergk "the three chief magistracies created in the year 367, two consuls and one praetor, were colleagues. When wars took place increasingly far away from Rome, the permanent presence of at least one magistrate with imperium in Rome was required. The praetor then lost his military function and the city of Rome was his usual field of action. Only then did the hierarchy of consuls and praetors emerge, and the cursus honorum was born."[552] Polybius described consuls as supreme commanders of the Roman army[553] but also "the consuls carried out a great number of civil tasks, mainly, but not only, during their stay in Rome at the beginning of the consular year after taking office, a stay that could last a few weeks or months depending on the changing circumstances of internal and external politics."[554] Consuls were also "head of Roman diplomacy ... they had to receive foreign embassies in the Urbs and introduce them, if necessary, in the senate".[555] Consuls "had legislative initiative, although the number of consular laws passed during the middle republc was relatively small and unquestionably smaller than that of tribunican laws; on occasion, the consuls were commissioned by the senate to conduct extraordinary investigations the consuls were responsible for and promoted many public works, such as a large number of the temples erected in Rome between the fourth and second centuries, and many roads built in Italy during that period; the consuls were responsible for appointing a dictator; and finally, they presided over the elections".[556] Consuls had ritual duties such as "public vows, the expiation of prodigies, and the appointment of the date for the celebration of the Feriae Latinae, as well as presidency over the festival at the Mons Albanus, presidency over the public games, and especially responsible for the proper performance of a ver sacrum."[557]

State treasury

Treasury called aerarium or aerarium Saturni (treasury of Saturn). This was used for "depositing cash and archives of the Roman state and was situated in the temple of Saturn below the Capitol. It was controlled by the quaestors under the general supervision of the Senate."[558] This treasury still existed during the empire period when revenues "were increasingly diverted into the fiscus (imperial treasury). The aerarium eventually became the treasury of the city of Rome."[559]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥ In 367, Lex Licinia Sextia passed: plebeian class were now able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. one of the consulships.[560] This in effect throughout rest of Republican period

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ widespread ♥ "In 67 B.C. the tribune C. Cornelius passed a law compelling praetors to follow their own edicts. The tribune's aim probably was to prevent praetors' ad hoc deviations in the administration of civil (perhaps also provincial) law prompted by favoritism or spite."[562]
♠ Bribery ♣ widespread ♥ e.g. claims of Cicero and others about abuse of Catiline while governor in Africa. Likely exaggerated, but still seem to have some basis in activities of elites at time (cf. Cicero, "In Catilinam", Sallust De Catilinae coniuratione)[563]
♠ Abuse of power ♣ widespread ♥ "In 67 B.C. the tribune C. Cornelius passed a law compelling praetors to follow their own edicts. The tribune's aim probably was to prevent praetors' ad hoc deviations in the administration of civil (perhaps also provincial) law prompted by favoritism or spite."[564]

♠ Professionalization of state-level bureaucracy ♣ absent ♥ officials at this period still 'volunteer' elites[565]

♠ Promotion of state-level officials to other positions ♣ absent ♥ officials often held numerous posts during lifetime, but these were separately earned (not promoted by executive or anyone else)
' Cursus honorum: quaestor, aedile "not obligatory", praetor, consul, "and finally" censor."[566] "Before starting on a political career, young men were expected to have spent at least ten years in a military post (military tribune or legate ...) or sometimes in a legal career in the courts. Magistracies were then sought, usually in a particular order - the cursus honorum (cours eof honors). This was fixed by law in 180 BC, although not everyone followed the normal career pattern".[567] Curule magistrates: praetors, consuls, censors, curule aediles.[568]

Magistracies were then sought, usually in a particular order - the cursus honorum (cours eof honors). Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 15 ♥ 13 provinces, plus Italy divided into roughly 2 'zones' (Rome and the Italian coloniae, municipiae, and allied cities (socii)) 241 BC - Sicilia (Sicily) taken over from the Carthaginians and annexed at the end of the First Punic War. 237 BC - Corsica et Sardinia; these two islands were taken over from the Carthaginians and annexed soon after the Mercenary War, in 238 BC and 237 BC respectively. 197 BC - Hispania Citerior; along the east coast of the (Iberian Peninsula); part of the territories taken over from the Carthaginians . 197 BC - Hispania Ulterior; along the southern coast of the (Iberian Peninsula); part of the territories taken over from the Carthaginians in the Second Punic War. 147 BC - Macedonia; mainland Greece. It was annexed after a rebellion by the Achaean League. 146 BC - Africa; modern day Tunisia and western Libya; home territory of Carthage; annexed after the destruction of Carthage in the Third Punic War. 129 BC - Asia; formerly the Kingdom of Pergamon in western Anatolia (modern Turkey) by its last king, Attalus III, in 133 BC. 120 BC - Gallia Narbonensis (southern France); prior to its annexation it was called Gallia Transalpina (Gallia the other side of the Alps) to distinguish it from Gallia Cisalpina (Gaul this side of the Alps, in northern Italy). It was annexed following attacks on the allied Greek city of Massalia (Marseille). 67 BC - Creta et Cyrenae; Cyrenaica was bequeathed to Rome in 78 BC. However, it was not organised as a province. It was incorporated into the province of Creta et Cyrenae when Crete was annexed in 67 BC. 63 BC - Pontus et Bithynia; the Kingdom of Bithynia (in North-western Anatolia - Turkey) was bequeathed to Rome by its last king, Nicomedes IV in 74 BC. It was organised as a Roman province at the end of the Third Mithridatic War (73-63 BC) by Pompey who incorporated the eastern part of the defeated Kingdom of Pontus into it in 63 BC. 63 BC - Syria; Pompey annexed Syria at the end of the Third Mithridatic War. 58 BC - Cilicia et Cyprus; Cilicia was created as a province in the sense of area of military command in 102 BC in a campaign against piracy. The Romans controlled only a small area. In 74 BC Lycia and Pamphylia (to the east) were added to the smal Roman possessions in Cilicia. Cilicia came fully under Roman control towards the end of the Third Mithridatic War - 73-63 BC. The province was reorganised by Pompey in 63 BC. Cyprus was annexed and added to this province in 58 BC. 46 BC - Africa Nova (eastern Numidia - Algeria), Julius Caesar annexed eastern Numidia and the new province called Africa Nova (new Africa) to distinguish it from the older province of Africa, which become known as Africa Vetus (Old Africa).


♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥

"In the republic governors of provinces were appointed by the Senate and were originally changed annually. Governors were responsbile for law and order, security, administration of justice and the collection of taxes. As Rome expanded its territory, a consul or praetor could expect to become a provincial governor (both called proconsul) at the end of his term of office, giving him money-making opportunities in the provinces. The imperium of a governor was restricted to his province."[572] Governor had a financial secretary (quaestor) and legati advisers ("normally senators chosen by the Senate on advice from the governor"). They also had amici (friends and advisors). The quaestor took over as quaestor pro praetor if the governor died or left the province.[573]

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ widespread ♥ "In 67 B.C. the tribune C. Cornelius passed a law compelling praetors to follow their own edicts. The tribune's aim probably was to prevent praetors' ad hoc deviations in the administration of civil (perhaps also provincial) law prompted by favoritism or spite."[580]
♠ Bribery ♣ widespread ♥ e.g. claims of Cicero and others about abuse of Catiline while governor in Africa. Likely exaggerated, but still seem to have some basis in activities of elites at time (cf. Cicero, "In Catilinam", Sallust De Catilinae coniuratione)[581]
♠ Abuse of power ♣ widespread ♥ Especially after unification of Italy 272 BCE up until Augustus ended the practice, "Tax farmers were often utilized to collect provincial taxes." Problems with tax farmers, as outlined by A. H. M. Jones (1968) included "Oppression and extortion [which] began very early in the provinces and reached fantastic proportions in the later republic. ... There was usually collusion between the governor and the tax contractors and the senate was too far away to exercise any effective control over either."[582] As outlined by A. H. M. Jones (1968) "The other great abuse of the provinces was extensive moneylending at exorbitant rates of interest to the provincial communities, which could not raise enough ready cash to satisfy both the exorbitant demands of the tax contractors and the blackmail levied by the governors."[583]

♠ Professionalization of local bureaucracy ♣ inferred absent ♥ "In the republic governors of provinces were appointed by the Senate and were originally changed annually. Governors were responsbile for law and order, security, administration of justice and the collection of taxes. As Rome expanded its territory, a consul or praetor could expect to become a provincial governor (both called proconsul) at the end of his term of office, giving him money-making opportunities in the provinces. The imperium of a governor was restricted to his province."[584] Governor had a financial secretary (quaestor) and legati advisers ("normally senators chosen by the Senate on advice from the governor"). They also had amici (friends and advisors). The quaestor took over as quaestor pro praetor if the governor died or left the province.[585]

♠ modal number of years local bureaucrats spend in service ♣ ♥

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ absent ♥ A formal legal code was first founded in the Twelve Tables of 450-449 BCE. Law thereafter was based on precedent. Our sources of knowledge of Roman law include the forensic speeches of Cicero; the Institutes of Gaius textbook (from 160 CE); and, much later, the sixth century CE Corpus Ius Civilis of Justinian. Wax tablets and papyri (contracts and wills etc.) also provide information on Roman law. [586] Professional judges did not exist until the Roman Dominate although at that time their precise role vis-a-vis that of Imperial officials is a matter of debate. [587] Before this time there were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[588] According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[589] However, whilst mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[590]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ absent ♥

During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [591] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[592] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[593]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[594] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[595] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ present ♥Magistrates had "executive, judicial, legislative, diplomatic, military and even religious functions."[596] including prosecuting criminal violations

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ This period saw an expansion of access to the legal system to non-citizens, suggesting that all citizens still retained the right to access to the system that they enjoyed in previous periods. "In the last two to three cents. bc, however, the expansion of Rome's commerce and empire over the Mediterranean world (see imperialism, roman) made it impossible to maintain the exclusiveness of the old civil law. New, informal institutions appeared, which depended on the intention of the parties rather than the observance of external forms. An important example is the class of agreements binding by consent alone (consensual contracts: See contract), which provided a way of enforcing the principal commercial transactions, such as sale, lease, and partnership. These new institutions were open to foreigners and citizens alike (see ius gentium). The form of stipulatio, which could be used to make any lawful agreement legally binding, was also widened so that it was now open to foreigners. Special boards of assessors (recuperatores) had early been set up for disputes with non-citizens, and about 242 bc a special magistrate for matters involving foreigners (praetor peregrinus; see praetor (republic)) was created with jurisdiction over these cases."[597] The following text, which was originally the supporting text provided for this "present" code, is in fact only tangentially relevant, and is missing some references, but it is preserved in order to keep a record of the variable's coding history. During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [598] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[599] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[600]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[601] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[602] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ present ♥

The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[636]

From c300 BCE "Although the pontiffs continued to be extremely important as expert interpreters of the law, both before and after the passage of the Ogulnian Law, Cn. Flavius's actions [publication of the legis actiones and the legal calendar] simply removed any lingering pretense that the patrician college of pontiffs exercised a monopolistic control of the civil law. Like the increase in number of priests responsible for consulting the Sibylline Books in 368 B.C., this augmentation resulted from the fact that in Roman aristocratic culture these prominent priesthoods were viewed as being akin to public magistracies and as bestowing similar status and prestige. Roman society was becoming increasingly secularized, Roman religion was coming more and more under the influence of Greek culture, and these two trends were seriously eroding the traditional patrician claim to special expert religious knowledge. High-ranking plebeians, who were peers of the most prominent patricians in every other way, were eager to become members of these exclusive religious clubs and thereby to top off their public careers with the added distinction of a priestly office."[637]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ “The basis of the Roman economy was farming and land ownership.” [638]

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [639]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ We can infer that exclusivity existed, but communal holdings existed as well. “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [640] “The co-residence of relatives and their sharing of land and other resources diminished the impact of the withdrawal part of adult laborers from peasant farms.” [641]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ “If in your will you wished to include specific gifts of money or property to people other than the heir, you had to add a line called the legacy in which you described the property or amount of money and the person to whom you wanted it to go.” [642]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [643]
♠ Lease Rights ♣ absent ♥ No official legal rights for renters until late medieval Italy. [644]
♠ Transfer of property ♣ ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [645]
♠ Use of property ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned in sources. Farming was mainly subsistence farming. [646]
♠ Property market ♣ absent ♥ “During the Late Republic, when rich men began buying up all the land and putting together huge estates, the Romans passed legislation stipulating that it was illegal for any one person to own more than 500 iugera of land. In practice, this law was plainly ignored or else people found loopholes to circumvent it.” [647] There is no evidence of a property market in Rome until the late Middle Ages.

♠ Other property ownership ♣ present ♥ “Slaves were regarded as property, just like any other object owned by their master.” [648]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ “If in your will you wished to include specific gifts of money or property to people other than the heir, you had to add a line called the legacy in which you described the property or amount of money and the person to whom you wanted it to go.” [649]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ “It has been held since the early nineteenth century that the control of property by fathers of families at the time of the Twelve Tables had evolved from a system of ownership by villages (pagi) or clans." [650]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ Women could not sell or transfer their land. “The patria potestas extended to questions of life and death, as was recognized by the Laws of the Twelve Tables (ca. 450 B.c.), as well over the sons of the family as over the women; cf. Gaius Institutes of Roman Law ii. 96: 'It should be noted that nothing can be granted in the way of justice to those in potestate, i.e., to slaves, children, and wives; for it is reasonable to conclude that, since these persons can own no property, they are incompetent to claim anything in point of law'...Through this change in the marriage customs, nuptiae solo consensu contrahuntur, the husband had use of the dos only, in his wife's property; and that ceased if the marriage was broken off. In other words, the Roman matron was to all intents and purposes a free agent, controlling her own actions and to some extent her own property; for the control of the gens still prevented her from alienating that either by sale or by testament.” [651]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ present ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [652] “If in your will you wished to include specific gifts of money or property to people other than the heir, you had to add a line called the legacy in which you described the property or amount of money and the person to whom you wanted it to go.” [653]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ inferred absent ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [654]
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ “As time went on, the Romans became concerned that too many people were leaving their property to persons other than their children.” [655]
♠ Trusts ♣ ♥
♠ State expropriation ♣ inferred frequent ♥ “After Sulla’s army had successfully fought king Mithradates in the East (88-84) and, on their return to Italy, had defeated his political opponents (83-82) who had taken possession of Rome in his absence, his soldiers were the first veterans to receive land on a large scale. They received the land that was taken from the Italian communities that had supported Sulla’s enemies." 31: “Octavian had to requisition land from communities in Italy and elsewhere on a large scale, thereby causing widespread hardship and poverty.” [656]
♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ ♥ “Only exceptionally would a god directly claim a plot of land. That might happen by a lightning strike leaving a visible mark in the soil. The strip of land would be marked off by a miniature fence or boxlike structure bearing the inscription fulgar conditum (‘covered lightening-trace’). The owner would hardly lose more than a square foot” [657]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ present ♥ "Roman marriage was monogamous. A full Roman marriage could occur only if both parties were Roman citizens or had been granted conubium."[658]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ absent ♥ "Roman marriage was monogamous. A full Roman marriage could occur only if both parties were Roman citizens or had been granted conubium."[659]
EALT:From a glossary of Latin legal terms: iustum matrimonium "legal marriage, marriage made in accordance with Roman law. Also called instae nuptiae".[660] From a glossary of Latin legal terms: stuprum "illicit sex, punishable under the lex Julia de adulteriis. This included adultery (sexual relations between a married woman and someone other than her husband), as well as sexual relations with an unmarried woman or virgin of respectable status."[661] lex Julia refers to 23 BCE "in early Roman law, if a woman cohabited with a man for one full year, she was regarded as having entered the man's manus by a kind of usucapio [taking by use]".[662]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ inferred present ♥ From a glossary of Latin legal terms: concubinatus "a non-legal but long-term sexual relationship, usually between an unmarried man and a woman of lower status with whom iustum matrimonium was either legally impossible or socially inappropriate".[663]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ inferred absent ♥ From a glossary of Latin legal terms: concubinatus "a non-legal but long-term sexual relationship, usually between an unmarried man and a woman of lower status with whom iustum matrimonium was either legally impossible or socially inappropriate".[664]
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ ♥


Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ yes ♥ "juristic innovation contained in the Twelve Tables ... A woman who was in the manus of her husband was basically in the same legal position as her children, who were under the husband's parternal authority. ... If a woman did not enter the manus of her husband, she remained under the paternal authority of her father until he died, at which time she became legally independent. From the time of the Twelve Tables onwards, women who were not under their father's patria potestas or the manus of their husbands were still required to have a legal guardian (tutor) to assist or oversee their legal transactions. This duty was most often performed by a close male relative ... This institution was termed the perpetual guardianship of women (tutela perpetua mulierum), and the Vestal virgins were the only females in early Rome exempt from it (Gauis Institutes 1.145)."[665]
♠ Social class ♣ yes ♥ The plebeian class were able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. consulships certainly after 367 BCE,[666] but were excluded from the top Senatorial positions. Also, in the early republic plebeians were "forbidden to marry patricians".[667] "The proletarii at Rome were those citizens placed in the lowest property class who were exempt from military service and tributum and could serve the state only by contributing their children (proles) to it."[668]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ yes ♥ Non-Roman citizens could be excluded from the vote: Dentatus was a particularly aggressive general who conquered territories and incorporated its populations without giving them the vote.[669] "To the astonishment of Greek observers, a slave freed by a Roman citizens became a Roman citizen [and] Roman citizenship came to be available in due course not simply to members of Latin communities, but also to entire Italian peoples."[670]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ no ♥ The following refers to the imperial period, but suggests continuity with preceding periods. "Although women continued to be barred from public office and from taking legal action on their own behalf, they gained considerable independence from male authority in their private lives."[671] There were full-time priestesses, the Vestals and the priestess of Ceres and Proserpina who were supported by the state.[672] The latter roles could not be said to be a bureaucratic position and their powers were not governmental: e.g. for Vestals "Wills and treaties were in their keeping, and they themselves could make a will. They could conduct business in their own name. They could give evidence in court without taking an oath. ... If they accidentally met a criminal on his way to execution, he was spared."[673]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ no ♥ The following refers to the imperial period, but suggests continuity with preceding periods. "Although women continued to be barred from public office and from taking legal action on their own behalf, they gained considerable independence from male authority in their private lives."[674]
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ yes ♥ "Some girls went to school, others were taught privately at home. They were an essential part of various religious rites. In these they were not silent ciphers but active participants, often needing physical stamina for long processions and mental effort to memorize and rehearse songs and ritual."[675]
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ no ♥ Likely very limited in this period. E.g. clear that Vestal priestesses could own property: "Wills and treaties were in their keeping, and they themselves could make a will. They could conduct business in their own name"[676]; "they could own and administer their own property".[677] However, this was not true for all other Roman women: "in early Roman law, if a woman cohabited with a man for one full year, she was regarded as having entered the man's manus by a kind of usucapio [taking by use]".[678] "A woman who was in the manus of her husband was basically in the same legal position as her children, who were under the husband's parternal authority. ... If a woman did not enter the manus of her husband, she remained under the paternal authority of her father until he died, at which time she became legally independent. From the time of the Twelve Tables onwards, women who were not under their father's patria potestas or the manus of their husbands were still required to have a legal guardian (tutor) to assist or oversee their legal transactions. This duty was most often performed by a close male relative ... This institution was termed the perpetual guardianship of women (tutela perpetua mulierum), and the Vestal virgins were the only females in early Rome exempt from it (Gauis Institutes 1.145)."[679] Overall, given that only a handful of females who were chosen for ritual functions were permitted to legally own their own property without the "perpetual guardianship" of a man it does not seem appropriate to code that property could be owned by women.

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ no: 509-368 BCE; yes: 366-264 BCE ♥ In 367, Lex Licinia Sextia passed: plebeian class were now able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. one of the consulships.[680] giving plebeians more rights including holding one consulship. "In 300, plebeians won the right to hold places in two of Rome's priestly colleges (the pontiffs and the augurs), both of which were enlarged."[681] Plebeians became pontiffs and augurs.[682] "In the early republic [plebeians] were excluded from the Senate and important priesthoods and had no part in the administration of the law."[683]

The carrot consisted primarily in the prospect of manumission itself, though it also involved the toleration of personal relationships (contubernia) among the slaves (familia), and the granting of separate property (peculium) for use on their own responsibility (Pólay 1967; Flory 1978; Friedl 1996: 75–85; Brinkhof 1978; Zeber 1981; Schumacher 2001a: 239–48, 265–76). The stick was put into concrete form with the withdrawal of these privileges, and rigorous (p. 594) punitive measures, ranging from corporal punishment to cruel execution (Schumacher 2001a: 276–91)."[693] The following is also worth noting. "To the astonishment of Greek observers, a slave freed by a Roman citizens became a Roman citizen [and] Roman citizenship came to be available in due course not simply to members of Latin communities, but also to entire Italian peoples."[694]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ Citizens and non-citizens had different rights, and some groups were initially granted citizenship without the right to vote, but sources do not suggest that any of this had anything to do with ethnicity or race; if anything, the implication is that any community of outsiders, and any enslaved person, regardless of race or ethnicity, could in theory become a citizen, and therefore enjoy the same rights as ethnic Romans, including standing for magistracies. "Roman citizenship came to possess two features which distinguished it from polis citizenship and which later surprised Greek observers: the automatic incorporation of freed slaves of Romans into the Roman citizen body; and the ease with which whole communities of outsiders could be admitted as citizens. By the time Rome faced the invasion of Hannibal in 218 bc, she had a long history of giving citizenship to Italian communities, either with the vote (optimo iure) or without the vote (sine suffragio). (The latter communities, as with Arpinum, the place of origin of C. Marius (1) and Cicero, were usually later granted the vote.) Apart from Roman communities of these two types and allies, socii, Italy also contained numerous Latin communities, whose members shared a number of rights with Romans, one of which was conubium. A child born to two Romans was a Roman; but so was a child born to a Roman father and a mother from a people possessing conubium. (See ius latii, Latini, municipium.)//"All citizens, after the abolition of the ban on conubium between patricians and plebeians, had conubium; they were also liable to tributum and military service. If they had the vote, they were also eligible to stand for magistracies. "[695] Previously coded "absent" or "no" on the basis of the fact that non-citizens could be excluded from the vote and from holding positions of authority. However, this claim was only partially referenced (the reference was to a source's mention of how a general named Dentatus did not grant the right to vote to the communities he incorporated into the Roman dominion[696]), and, as has been mentioned, the distinction between "citizen" and "non-citizen" does not seem to reflect ethnic discrimination.

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ "patricians - an elite class that closed their ranks to new members c. 500 B.C., soon after the expulsion of the Tarquins".[705] Elite status directly related to the public auspices ritual which "entailed the competence to request, observe, and announce Jupiter's signs regarding an important act and then to complete what was intended. Since auspication preceded every major action taken on the state's behalf, it formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power."[706] Elite positions became open to plebeian class but to a large degree elite status was hereditary. "the ascent of a family to the consulship over several generations was a common enough phenomenon; and a man who ennobled himself and his descendants by being the first of his family to achieve consulship or other high office was known as a novus homo, new man."[707] "Until 445 BCE patricians could not marry plebeians, and a free person could not marry a freedman or freedwoman until legistlation under Augustus made it permissible (except for senators)."[708]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ absent ♥
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ present ♥ "patricians - an elite class that closed their ranks to new members c. 500 B.C., soon after the expulsion of the Tarquins".[709] After monarchy patricians dominated "secular and sacred office."[710] "The power of the patricians was steadily eroded so that by c. 300 B.C. the political advantages of being a patrician were slight."[711] "High-ranking plebeians, who were peers of the most prominent patricians in every other way, were eager to become members of these exclusive religious clubs and thereby to top off their public careers with the added distinction of a priestly office."[712] Censors "assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property. This entire nexus of the comitia centuriata, census, tribal organization, and state taxation was attributed to King Servius Tullius by the ancient tradition."[713]

♠ social mobility ♣ present ♥ "The Ogulnian Law of 300 B.C. increased the number of pontiffs from four to eight and the number of augurs from four to nine, and ordained that the new priestly positions be filled by plebeians (Livy 10.6.2-9.2)."[714] "To the astonishment of Greek observers, a slave freed by a Roman citizens became a Roman citizen [and] Roman citizenship came to be available in due course not simply to members of Latin communities, but also to entire Italian peoples."[715] "Roman coloniae provided an avenue ... whereby men whose families were of Italian origin could achieve Roman citizenship without moving to Rome."[716] "in the second century Cato could assume that there were no formal barriers in the way of any Roman citizen achieving the highest office of the state."[717] "It was naturally rare for a man, none of whose family had ever held office, to reach the consulship, as did C. Marius and Cicero."[718] "In 342, a lex Genucia seems to have guaranteed the right of a plebeian to one of the consular posts in any given year."[719]

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ lower ♥ Manius Curius Dentatus (d. 270 BCE) "was unquestionably the most famous Roman of his day ... no ancestors of his are known to have held magistracies ... he himself went on to be consul three times (in 290, 275, and 274), thereby illustrating the opportunities open to plebeians."[720] Dentatus was a particularly aggressive general who conquered territories and incorporated its populations without giving them the vote.[721] "It was naturally rare for a man, none of whose family had ever held office, to reach the consulship, as did C. Marius and Cicero."[722]
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ present ♥ "the ascent of a family to the consulship over several generations was a common enough phenomenon; and a man who ennobled himself and his descendants by being the first of his family to achieve consulship or other high office was known as a novus homo, new man."[723]
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ ♥

♠ occupational mobility ♣ present ♥ During the 343-241 BCE period during which Rome was at war "in almost every year" Latin colonies that were established "allowed those who were impoverished the chance to make a new life." [724] Cash acquired through warfare used to build temples especially c.300 BCE, like the temple of Bellona (296 BCE), temple of Jupiter Victor (295 BCE), building programmes which "provided employment for the urban poor."[725] Likewise for public works projects such as the Appian Way and Appian Aqueduct after 312 BCE.[726] In the early republic plebeians were "forbidden to marry patricians" but could "participate in commerce, and some became very wealthy, forming the 'middle class' (equites) of Roman society."[727]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [10-30] ♥ Need to consult Scheidel "The Slave Population of Roman Italy" - slave population may be smaller than commonly suggested.

"The earliest known Roman code relating to slavery is the Twelve Tables, which dates to approxiately 450 B.C. During the republic and early empire, magisterial edicts, imperial orders, and occasionally senatorial decrees affected the structure, application, and evolution of Roman slave law. Though a few legal texts exist from the first two centuries A.D., no formal and comprehensive compilation of law, including laws pertaining to slavery, appeared before the Theodosian Code (Codex Theodosianus) was assembled in A.D. 435."[728]

"Slavery in the Roman Republic was fully institutionalized by 450 BCE, and was incorporated into the Twelve Tables legal code (451-450 BCE). Indeed, close to 700,000 prisoners of war were enslaved between 297 and 167 BCE in the Roman Empire. Altogether, millions of slaves inhabited ancient Rome, claiming 15-25 percent or even 35 percent of its population."[729]

"In the first century C.E. in Pergamon in Asia Minor, his home city, Galen estimated that there was a slave for every two adult citizens, so 33 percent; but in the rural villages of Egypt, the number was much lower, below 7 percent. Whereas the earlier Italian slave population had not exceeded 10 percent, Keith Hopkins has estimated that there were about two million slaves in the empire, equal to about 35 percent of the full population. There was significant downward mobility in the Roman empire!"[730]

"Hopkins (1978: 9, 102) estimated that by 31 B.C. there were at least two million slaves in Italy, out of a total population of six million."[731]

"According to Finley (1980), by the end of the Roman Republic, slaves comprised 30-35 percent of the population of Roman Italy, a figure comparable to that for the antebellum American South."[732]

Slaves were acquired through warfare.[733]

Mid-4th BCE: "One major change is likely to have been Rome's gradual transformation into a slave-owning society. Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8). It is worth nothing that this law was passed by the Roman army at Sutrium, the Latin colony on Rome's Etruscan frontier. It suggests that the army ratified this proposal either before or after a military campaign against non-Latin-speaking people, who must have been prime candidates for the Roman slave market."[734]

"That Roman agriculture in the late Republic was heavily dependent on the labour of slaves is well known, but it is not always appreciated that this phenomenon has its origin in the fourth century B.C."[735]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ Debt-bondage abolished in 326 or 313 BCE due to influx of slaves acquired through warfare.[736] Poetelian Law possibly c.326 or 313 BCE "banned or modified debt servitude."[737]

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ present ♥ "Slaves could be punished in any way by their owners. They were commonly beaten, as well as punished by branding and other mutilation. In the case of murder by a slave, all the owner's slaves could be executed (often by cruxifixion), largely to suppress possible revolts and encourage slaves to report plots."[743] "Slaves were routinely beaten for 'sport' and to relieve maters' frustrations. Until the time of Hadrian, Roman law permitted summary execution of slaves by their owners."[744]
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ inferred present ♥ "Slaves could be punished in any way by their owners. They were commonly beaten, as well as punished by branding and other mutilation. In the case of murder by a slave, all the owner's slaves could be executed (often by cruxifixion), largely to suppress possible revolts and encourage slaves to report plots."[745]
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ A law of 449 BCE "guaranteed the right of appeal to the people (the technical term was provocatio ad populum) against scourging or capital punishment by a magistrate. This reaffirmed a law supposedly passed in 509, the first year of the Republic."[746] It is known that a Vestal assistant in 178 BCE was scourged "for letting the sacred fire of her goddess go out".[747]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ "As for the Romans, Livy states that during the Punic Wars, they got so desperate after their defeat by Hannibal at Cannae that they buried alive two pairs of victims in the Forum Boarium: 'In the meantime, by the direction of the Books of Fate [the Sibylline books of oracles], some unusual sacrifices were offered; amongst others a Gaulish man and woman and a Greek man and woman were buried alive in the Cattle Market, in a place walled in with stone, which even before this time had been defiled with human victims, a sacrifice wholly alien to the Roman spirit.' (The earlier sacrifice occurred in 228 BCE when the Gauls were invading, and a later one apparently occurred ca. 114 after another military defeat in Illyria (see Gary Forsythe, A Critical History of Early Rome, pp 331-2). Pliny the Elder (28.3) also writes: 'Our own age even has seen a man and a woman buried alive in the Ox Market, Greeks by birth, or else natives of some other country with which we were at war at the time. The prayer used upon the occasion of this ceremonial, and which is usually pronounced first by the Master of the College of the Quindecimviri, if read by a person, must assuredly force him to admit the potency of [prayer] formulae; when it is recollected that it has been proved to be effectual by the experience of eight hundred and thirty years.'" [748]

♠ Elites ♣ [present; absent] ♥ Vestal priestesses who had broken their vow of chastity were buried alive: "One could argue that this was a punishment rather than a human sacrifice, but it was a ritualized killing enacted in order to atone to Vesta for the offense." [750] Also, sometimes Vestals were buried alive when no offence was known such as when a great catastrophe befell Rome (e.g. Gaulish invasion?) errant Vestals may have been assumed to have been partly the cause of the misfortune.
