Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ inferred present ♥ Taxes increased after unification of Italy 272 BCE. "In the provinces, however, the main form of tax was a tithe levied on communities, rather than directly on individuals."[932]

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ In Early Republic tax farmers "had the responsibility of converting provincial taxes, which were often collected in-kind, into hard cash."[933] Later: "Already by the 2nd century BC, Rome was receiving large quantities of grain as taxes in kind from some of its provinces. Initially much came from Siciliy and Sardinia, and then from Egypt and north Africa."[934]
♠ Labor ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention in sources consulted that taxes could be or were paid in labor. One might suggest this form of tax would have been unneeded due to the amount of slave labor present in Roman society. "Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8). It is worth nothing that this law was passed by the Roman army at Sutrium, the Latin colony on Rome's Etruscan frontier. It suggests that the army ratified this proposal either before or after a military campaign against non-Latin-speaking people, who must have been prime candidates for the Roman slave market."[935]
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects."[936] As inflation increased 260s BCE there was "widespread hoarding, and the government refused to accept coinage for many taxes, insisting instead on payment in kind."[937]


Debt and Indemnities

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ ♥
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ inferred present ♥ Taxes increased after unification of Italy 272 BCE. "In the provinces, however, the main form of tax was a tithe levied on communities, rather than directly on individuals."[938]

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ [1-3] ♥

The Roman Republic had no independent central bureaucracy; it was not until the empire period that revenues were diverted into an imperial treasury.[939] There were state officials in the form of elected quaestors and censors who were directly involved in the taxation system. Quaestors were "financial and administrative officials who maintained public records, administered the treasury (aerarium), acted as paymasters accompanying generals on campaigns and were financial secretaries to governors."[940] A duty of the censors "was to conduct a census of the Roman people, which involved taking statements under oath from adult male heads of households as to the members of their family and their property holdings; on the basis of these formal declarations, the censors assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property."[941]

"The only direct tax paid by Romans was the tributum, a land and property tax levied in times of emergency. This was abolished on land in Italy in 167 BC as the treasury was enriched through overseas conquests."[942] "In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects."[943] tributa / stipendia were "taxes raised on the basis of a census or tax list. ... these tributa were not levied on Roman citizens, but only in exceptional circumstances, as in war. After 167 bc, there were no longer such tributa (Plin. HN 33.56), only in rare emergency situations ..."[944] "In the Republic and Principate, the main tributa were the tributum soli (land tax) and the tributum capitis (poll tax) (cf. the essential study Neesen 1980; discussion and corrections in Brunt 1981 / 1990). Like all tributa, they were calculated on the basis of a census list."[945]

"In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects. The basic rate was just .01 percent, although occasionally rising to .03 percent. It was assessed principally to pay the army during war. In fact, afterwards the tax was often rebated (Jones 1974: 161). It was levied directly on individuals, who were counted at periodic censuses."[946] Taxes increased after unification of Italy 272 BCE. "In the provinces, however, the main form of tax was a tithe levied on communities, rather than directly on individuals."[947]

During the Republican period there is not much evidence for taxation on trade. "New indirect taxes in the empire included portoria (customs). There were also taxes on the sale and manumission of slaves and inheritance taxes, levied at a rate of 5 percent. The inheritance tax was levied on the property of Roman citizens only, and the revenue went to the aerarium militare (military treasury), together with the revenues from the 1 percent tax on auction sales. In the imperial provinces taxes were collected by a procurator, and in sensatorial provinces the quaestor was in charge, although publicani (tax collectors or publicans) were used."[948] "There were two types of customs duty to be collected at Rome, the ansarium and the foricul(i)arium, although it must be noted that these taxes are attested only in a handful of inscriptions dating from the final quarter of the second century and the first half of the third century AD; these inscriptions are found on stones intended to mark out the limits of the customs boundary."[949] In the first century AD "Pliny reports that there were thirty-seven customs gates or portae in Vespasianic Rome"."[950] "vectigal, etymologically deriving from vehere (to convey or transport), first designated the cartloads of crops from ager publicus (public land) that had to be given to the state as landlord by the leaseholder. As a consequence, only the ratio could be defined in advance, but not the exact amount, which depended on the harvest, and this concept later defined also the taxes called vectigalia: they were not raised on the basis of (census) lists but on occasion."[951]

296 CE Diocletian's tax system made collection in kind (corn, clothes, wine) more common "to supply the army and pay the civil servants. This tax included the annona militaris. The new system of collection of tax in kind was based on land (iugatio) and on the population (capitatio), and was based on the area of cultivable land (iugum) that could be worked by one man (caput), providing him with subsistence."[952]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ "In the Republic and Principate, the main tributa were the tributum soli (land tax) and the tributum capitis (poll tax) (cf. the essential study Neesen 1980; discussion and corrections in Brunt 1981 / 1990). Like all tributa, they were calculated on the basis of a census list."[953] "In the early republic state income was obtained from rents for the ager publicus. The only direct tax paid by Romans was the tributum, a land and property tax levied in times of emergency. This was abolished on land in Italy in 167 BC as the treasury was enriched through overseas conquests. The tributumm continued to be paid by citizens and non-citizens in conquered territory and was an important source of revenue. It consisted of tributum soli (land and property tax) and tributum capitis (poll tax)."[954]
♠ Property ♣ present ♥ "The censors were always two in number and held office for eighteen months. Their primary duty was to conduct a census of the Roman people, which involved taking statements under oath from adult male heads of households as to the members of their family and their property holdings; on the basis of these formal declarations, the censors assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property. This entire nexus of the comitia centuriata, census, tribal organization, and state taxation was attributed to King Servius Tullius by the ancient tradition."[955] "In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects. The basic rate was just .01 percent, although occasionally rising to .03 percent. It was assessed principally to pay the army during war. In fact, afterwards the tax was often rebated (Jones 1974: 161). It was levied directly on individuals, who were counted at periodic censuses."[956]
♠ People ♣ present ♥ Mid-4th BCE: "One major change is likely to have been Rome's gradual transformation into a slave-owning society. Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8). It is worth nothing that this law was passed by the Roman army at Sutrium, the Latin colony on Rome's Etruscan frontier. It suggests that the army ratified this proposal either before or after a military campaign against non-Latin-speaking people, who must have been prime candidates for the Roman slave market."[957]
♠ Trade ♣ [absent; present] ♥ "There were two types of customs duty to be collected at Rome, the ansarium and the foricul(i)arium, although it must be noted that these taxes are attested only in a handful of inscriptions dating from the final quarter of the second century and the first half of the third century AD; these inscriptions are found on stones intended to mark out the limits of the customs boundary."[958] In the first century AD "Pliny reports that there were thirty-seven customs gates or portae in Vespasianic Rome"."[959] no evidence for taxes on trade but it is hard to believe that, although Roman taxes were light and there was no large bureaucracy to fund, there were none at all - building of the Salt Road encouraged trade.
♠ Income ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention in sources consulted so far for direct taxes on income. "The only direct tax paid by Romans was the tributum, a land and property tax levied in times of emergency. This was abolished on land in Italy in 167 BC as the treasury was enriched through overseas conquests."[960]
♠ Production ♣ ♥ "vectigal, etymologically deriving from vehere (to convey or transport), first designated the cartloads of crops from ager publicus (public land) that had to be given to the state as landlord by the leaseholder. As a consequence, only the ratio could be defined in advance, but not the exact amount, which depended on the harvest, and this concept later defined also the taxes called vectigalia: they were not raised on the basis of (census) lists but on occasion."[961]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ "The only direct tax paid by Romans was the tributum, a land and property tax levied in times of emergency. This was abolished on land in Italy in 167 BC as the treasury was enriched through overseas conquests."[962] "In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects."[963] tributa / stipendia were "taxes raised on the basis of a census or tax list. ... these tributa were not levied on Roman citizens, but only in exceptional circumstances, as in war. After 167 bc, there were no longer such tributa (Plin. HN 33.56), only in rare emergency situations ..."[964] "In the Republic and Principate, the main tributa were the tributum soli (land tax) and the tributum capitis (poll tax) (cf. the essential study Neesen 1980; discussion and corrections in Brunt 1981 / 1990). Like all tributa, they were calculated on the basis of a census list."[965]
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ Taxes increased after unification of Italy 272 BCE. "In the provinces, however, the main form of tax was a tithe levied on communities, rather than directly on individuals. Local communities would decide for themselves how to divide up the tax burden among their citizens (Goffart 1974: 11)."[966] Until Augustus, "Tax farmers were often utilized to collect provincial taxes."[967]
♠ state officials ♣ inferred present ♥ The Roman Republic had no independent central bureaucracy; it was not until the empire period that revenues were diverted into an imperial treasury.[968] There were state officials in the form of elected quaestors and censors who were directly involved in the taxation system. Quaestors were "financial and administrative officials who maintained public records, administered the treasury (aerarium), acted as paymasters accompanying generals on campaigns and were financial secretaries to governors."[969] A duty of the censors "was to conduct a census of the Roman people, which involved taking statements under oath from adult male heads of households as to the members of their family and their property holdings; on the basis of these formal declarations, the censors assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property."[970]

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ In Early Republic tax farmers "had the responsibility of converting provincial taxes, which were often collected in-kind, into hard cash."[971] Later: "Already by the 2nd century BC, Rome was receiving large quantities of grain as taxes in kind from some of its provinces. Initially much came from Siciliy and Sardinia, and then from Egypt and north Africa."[972]
♠ Labor ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention in sources consulted that taxes could be or were paid in labor. One might suggest this form of tax would have been unneeded due to the amount of slave labor present in Roman society. "Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8). It is worth nothing that this law was passed by the Roman army at Sutrium, the Latin colony on Rome's Etruscan frontier. It suggests that the army ratified this proposal either before or after a military campaign against non-Latin-speaking people, who must have been prime candidates for the Roman slave market."[973]
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "In the earliest days of the Republic Rome's taxes were quite modest, consisting mainy of a wealth tax on all forms of property, including land, houses, slaves, animals, money and personal effects."[974] As inflation increased 260s BCE there was "widespread hoarding, and the government refused to accept coinage for many taxes, insisting instead on payment in kind."[975]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥

♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥

"Another lex Licinia Sextia passed in 367 limited the amount of public land that could be held by an individual to 500 iugera. It seems that some patricians had been holding far more than this amount."[976]

In 500 BCE "the landholdings even of the richest Romans" were, compared to the situation in the 1st century BCE "much smaller, and the owners themselves will have taken part in farming them."[977]

In the early republic plebeians were "forbidden to marry patricians" but could "participate in commerce, and some became very wealthy, forming the 'middle class' (equites) of Roman society."[978]
